ROBERTS IS ANXIOUS.
Robert W. Roberts, president of the
Board of Election Commissioners, in
an interview last night stated:
The time has come when either the Inno
cence or guilt of the Election Commissioners
must bo definitely settled. Ws have been as
sailed by a designing press that has stopped
at nothing to heap discredit upon the efforts
of the commission to give the community a
pure ballot and clean administration of its
election affairs. A cowed and partisan Grand
Jury has (prostituted its power to inaugurate
a legal proceeding that has failed and no stone
has b?*n left unturned to Injure the present
city administration by besmirching the Elec
tion Commissioners and piling false charges
up against them.
We have waited patiently and In rain for
some sort of a public trial that would settle
the matter one wag or the other. This has
been denied us to date, for the Grand Jury
would not hear our side of the story and the
press is devoted to but one phase of the situ
ation. We have, therefore, demanded a public
Investigation and are prepared to stand by the
results. We have nothing to conceal or to be
ashamed of. I
Following is a copy of the request
for trial as made by the members of
the commission:
To the Honorable Eugene E, Schmlts, Mayor
of the City and County of San FranciscoâWe.
the undersigned, members of the Board of
Election Commissioners of the City and County
of San Francisco, hereby represent to your
Honor that certain charges have been made
against us and each of us, accusing us of
willful and corrupt misconduct In our*said
office, as said Election Commissioners, and we
and each of us hereby represent that you, as
said Mayor of tbe city and county, by virtue
of the powers imposed upon you by the charter

PLAN TO FIGHT
TOBACCO TRUST
Independent Manufacturers
and Dealers Organize to
Wage the Trade Campaign
The tobacco trust finds guns of war
leveled at it in San Francisco. There
is no capitulation to it in its move to
take the city for wholesale and retail
trade in tobacco. The independent
manufacturers, wholesalers and store
keepers are preparing to resist to the
starvation point, they say, the cam
paign to make them yield.
They met last night, about 250 In
number, all enthusiastic for the fray.
They revived the Cigar and Tobacco
Merchants' Association, effected a new
organization and made plans to fight
the trust.
J. S. Parry announced that he had
taken st ;>s to deal the "vulture" a
smashing blow. He will have a bill in
troduced in the Legislature prohibiting
the giving away of coupons or certltb
cates with tobaccos, cigars and cigar
ettes.
The independents are to retard the
sale of the American Tobacco Com
pany's goods by appeal to their cus
tomers. The millions of the trust are a
drop in the bucket, say the independ
ents, if the independents will now
stand together.
The following were the officers elect
ed to lead in the great war of smoke:
President, J. S. Parry; first vice rre.Â«ident,
E5. W. Brigge; second vice president, William
Harrison; third vice president. M. Coblentz;
secretary, I.eon S. Mayer; treasurer. Slg. Ca
hen; directors (retailers) âHenry Harris. H.
Wolf, Dan P. Carter, Ben Adler. Max Cahen;
cigar manufacturersâD. Frenckel and S.
Hoffman; tobacco manufacturersâJ. R.
Blanchard and T. J. Linehan; leaf tobacco
dealer, Albert Kohlberg; cigarette manufac
turer. H. Bonis; city salesmenâEd Allenberg
and K. Bailie; wholesalersâMilton S. Esberg
and H. Bier.
In his speech accepting the presi
dency Parry declared that he would
serve only on the assurance that he
had the unswerving support of "the
cigar dealers who own their own stores
and their own souls." He said:
We are going to have a long, hard and seri
ous fight and must have determined support.
With tha strong backing of our association, I
am willing to take oft my coat, go to the bat
and give the best that's In me for the fight.
Never mind about a speech from meâit is
action that we want.
Remarks were made by several Oak
land dealers, who are threatened by
the American Tobacco Company, and
a desire to co-operate with the San
Francisco organization in hearty fash
ion was expressed- All those called on
were hopeful that the trust could be
successfully combated.
Donations from leading Independent
firms to the cause were reported to the
amount of $550.
E. W. Brlggs urged the independent
dealers to stand together. He had
words of condemnation for those who
had "sold out to the trust." He spoke
in praise of M. A. Gunst & Co. and of
C. V. Poar for "standing between tho
If every American article were as Rood as Sorosis shoes.
Uncle Sam would supply the whole world with its merchandise.
It is well known that the American manufacturer sells his
product abroad at a lower price than he charges at home.
Cheap cost of production in Europe forces him to cut the
price.
But Sorosis shoes sell at a premium in Europe. Their
merit commands it.
Neither low-priced nor high-priced shoes can compete with
the lasting beauty and comfort of Sorosis. It stands alone as
the acme of good shoemaking.
In England Sorosis shoes cost $4.00 a pair; in Germany
and Canada $4.50; in France and Russia $5.00.

216 POST THIRD STREET
LOOKS FORWARD
TO A BIG YEAR
Banker Hutton of New York
Is Firm in Belief That
1905 Will Be Prosperous
â¢ No one takes a more hopeful view of
the commercial conditions of this coun
try for the coming year than Edward
F. Hutton, a well-known New York
banker, who is among the latest ar
rivals at the St. Francis. Banker Hut
ton is making a short visit to San
Francisco on private business. In dis
cussing the commercial affairs of-the
country last evening he said:
During the last thirty days activity on the
New. York Stock Exchange has been pro
nounced and the transactions have been the
greatest since the bull market of 1901. It
was but a natural result, after a continued
advance in the price of securities for six
months with very lew reactions, ana those or
small moment, that a time would arrive when
adverse news would bring aboqt a material re
action. In fact, speculation had begun to as
sume such proportions at the opening of De
cember that many of the leading financial In
stitutions in New York were hoisting danger
signals. Banks were scrutinizing collateral
more carefully and there was a general feel
ing that a reaction must come before the flm
of the new year. It came, however, most un
expectedly, as Is usually the case, shortly
after the Issuance of the President's message,
and on Wednesday and Thursday, December (1
and 7. liquidation was severe and at times
panicky. The same large interests that
brought about the reaction appeared as buyers
of stocks on Thursday, and they have been ac
cumulating them on all weak days since then.
The technical position of the market is now
a strong one and I look for a resumption cf
the uoward trend of securities without any
great reaction for some time. It has been
frequently observed that "as .gees iron and
steel, so goes the country." The production
and consumption of pig Iron is rapidly in
creasing. The large steel companies are re
fusing orders for delivery beyond June next
and prices are being raised on all finished
products. It Is the general opinion that the
exports of merchandise from the United States
for 1904 will exceed 5000.000.000. which breaks
the high record of 1901 over $60,000,000.
In considering railroad earnings It Is well to
keep in mind that during the last six months
the railroad companies of this country bay*
expended hundreds of millions of dollars for
betterments. The effect of these expenditures
will ultimately become apparent In the reduced
operating expenses, and it is also reasonable to
expect that before long these extraordinary ex
penditures will cease. Then the true earning
power cf the big railroads will be shown and
result in the payments of dividends where
none are now being paid and increased divi
dends on many roads now on the market list.
With enormous corn and cotton crops and
high prices for wheat, an increased production
of gold and enlarged exports of our manufac
tured roods I look forward to the year 190R
with a strong belief In an improvement in all
lines of business, and. as a natural result, a
general advance in prices of securities ana
better conditions for the people in - all sec
tions of ths country-
trust and the Independent dealers. Let
us not be frightened like sheep, but
let us give battle and this wolf of a
trust will turn out to be an ass in
wolf's skin."
Other speakers were Herman Gud
stadt, M., Cerf. Charles Mattheas and
President Parry.

PLUNGER YEAGER TO RACE HERE'
SCHOOLMATE DEFEATS HONITON
FOR THE HANDICAP AT OAKLAND
Winner Is Bidden by Davis
and Nearly Equals a
Coast Record.
ISHTAR AT LONG ODDS
sir Wilfred Captures the
Kindergarten Event in
Commanding Fashion.
BY FRED E. Ml LHOLLAND.
, "Kansas'* Price, who watches selling
races as a cat does a mouse, in search
of sweet singers swinging idly in their
cages, turned loose a tartar in the mile
and seventy yard handicap yesterday
at Oakland.
His Wadsworth 4 gelding Schoolmate
appeared for the first time in California
dressed in his best, und, handling the
196 pounds assigned him with the ease
that a monkey juggles cocoanuts, de
feated the favorite, Honiton, by half
a length. The latter mare shouldered
top weight, 118 pounds, and ran a re
rr.arkabl.v good race, even though
btaten somewhat handily at the finish.
Travers took her out in the lead and
as the track was still somewhat heavy
the riders invariably picked a course
fully fifteen feet from the rail. Under
those adverse conditions she lost by
less than a length in 1:44%, within half
a second of the coast record.
SCHOOLMATE WELL HANDLED.
The winner. Schoolmate, perfectly
ridden by \Y. Davis, was never worse
thai) third and had the first choice at
his mercy when the stretch was
reached. Ara ran an indifferent third.
Magrane showed a twinkle of early
speed. Leila and Stilicho made no
showing whatever. As good as 10 to 1
could be had about the winner.
Favorite players again spent an un
easy session, for the ' baby" racer Sir
Wilfred, a 7 to 10 chance alone won
brackets. Handy Bill, Budd Wade,
Redan and Ishtar, In addition to
Schoolmate, dropped in at lengthy quo
tations.
Handy Bill made a show of Dei
farina, the 13 to 20 favorite for the
opening seven furlong selling number.
There was a genteel play on Bill at
4 and 5 to 1. and with Willie Daly up
he led from start to finish. The favor
ite disposed of Baker by three lengths
for the place. The other starters were
outclassed-
At his second essay D. Cameron's Sir
Wilfred made good. The son of Ossary
and Plumeria went to the post a 7 to
10 choice and spreadeagled his field.
Soledad, the Napa Stock Farm entry,
piloted by Bullman, held the balance
of the field safe, leading out Abe Meyer
half a length for place honors. Dora
Gentry beat the barrier, leading for
about a sixteenth of a mile.
BUDD WADE WINS BY A NOSE.
Both of the choices, Lady Kent and
The Lieutenant, finished behind Budd
Wade in the mile selling affair. The
stable connections were sweet on Budd
yesterday and turned loose their money
with lavish hand in the ring. He had
O. Chandler In the saddle, and, after
looking an easy winner a furlong from
home, only downed Lady Kent, the
pacemaker, the shortest kind of a head.
Ihe Lieutenant finished a distant
third. Molto, a backed outsider, dis
played some early speed, but can't beat
this kind.
Bullman handed the Futurity course
sprint, fifth to be decided, with Redan,
an 8 to 1 outsider in the betting. He
got the sprinter away sailing and,
never in difficulties, breezed in ahead
of Joe Frey and Royal W T hlte, two
other neglected ones. Peter J, the fa
vorite, couldn't run fast enough to
raise a scrape. Berendos did not get
away from the barrier.
Rector, favorite, and Padua, second
choice, finished last and next to that
obhorred position, respectively, in the
abhorred position, respectively, in the
Bride, astride Ishtar, a 15 to 1 shot, led
almost from the start, winning cleverly
by a length from Judge and Little
Wally. Travers, on Rector, got away
poorly and could not break through
the blockade. Jones made a fine mess
Of it with Padua. 4

tional Tournament,
CHICAGO, Jan. 4.â W. K. Wright
of San Francisco has loomed up as
favorite for championship honors in
the coming: national amateur billiard]
tourney, which begins at the Chicago
Athletic Association tiie last of this
month. The Callfornian is being
picked to capture the event by many

tion Wright. In a letter to Colonel

the star of the tourney', whether he

proves most magnetic to lovers of bil
liards.

there ia little doubt that the time lim

admits this to be his best style, so the
combat should be doubly Interesting.

TWO ALLEGED FOOTPADS
ARE HELD BY POLICE
Man Points Out William Maguire
and Stephen Sarrell as Thugs
Who Robbed Pedestrian.
Stephen Sarrell and William Mac
guire, suspected footpads, were ar
rested In an East-street restaurant
last night by Detectives Mc/Jowan and
Conlon and placed in the tanks await
ing developments. The detectives say
they arrested the men on information
from a man whose identity they refuse
to disclose. \
The man claims he saw Sarrell and
Macguire hold up a pedestrian at
East and Harrison streets a few
nights ago. They were In the act of
going through his pockets, says the
informer, when a stranger hove in
sight and flashed a star, at sight of
which the two men fled. The police
think Sarrell and Macguire are the
men who have been holding up per
sons south of Market street lately.
ACCIDENT PUTS END
TO A HUNTING TRIP
Thomas Walsh and J. Ferguson Fall
From a Wagon and Suffer
Painful Injuries.
Thomas Walsh, the well-known sa
loon man, and J. Ferguson were the
victims of a wagon accident near Col
linsvllle on Tuesday night. Walsh, his
wife and Ferguson were thrown from
their vehicle while on a hunting trip.
Walsh and Ferguson each sustained a
broken arm, while Mrs. Walsh escaped
uninjured.
The accident occurred on a rough
stretch of road a few miles outside of
Collinsville. The wagon ran into a rut
and was upset, throwing its occupants
to the ground. The injured men were
brought back to this city last night.
Ferguson was removed to St Mary's
Hospital and Walsh to his home. Fer
guson received internal injuries that
may prove seripus. .

MOTIVE POWER
TO BE CHANGED
Works Board Grants Permit
for Conversion of the Cali
fornia Street Beach Line
I he Board of Works yesterday grant
ed a permit to the United Railroads to
change the present steam railroad from
California street and Ceneral avenue
to the Cliff House into an overhead
electric system over the same route.
Mrs. Emma L. Merritt, executrix of
the Sutro estate, withdrew her previous
protest against the granting of the
permit. Mrs. Merritt objected to a
double track on the Sutro property,
but City Engineer Woodward reported
that so far as the franchise for that
portion of the road is concerned it ap
pears to be optional with the owners
whether it is to have a single or double
track. The franchise for the road be
tween Parker and First avenues ex
pires in 1929 and the right to use elec
tricity thereon will be secured from the
Supervisors. The franchise between
First avenue and the beach will ex
pire in 1911. It Is understood that work
on the reconstruction of the road will
begin at once, all the material being
on hand.
The Supervisors' Street Committee
later recommended a resolution grant
ing the United Railroads permission to
use electricity on that portion of the
road on California street between Par
ker and First avenues- The committee
also favored the use of Iron poles on
the system.
The board ordered grades to be
changed on Fulton street from Twenty
fifth avenue to Thirty-seventh avenue.
Provision in the budget was made for
the expenditure of $15,000 for the im
provement of Fulton street from Twen
ty-fifth avenue to the Great Highway.
The appropriation is not sufficient to
carry out the entire improvement and
the matter of making grade changes
will be taken up separately.
The petitions of Albert Meyer to mac
adamize I street, between Twentieth
and Twenty-first avenues, and of A. B.
Clute to bituminize Sixth avenue, be
tween Clement and California streets,
were denied.
The board appointed John Casey
foreman in Chinatown, vice John But
ler transferred.
The petition of residents that the
premises at 2964 Twenty-second street
be condemned as a menace to life was
referred to the Health Board.
The City Architect's plans for a two
story frame engine-house on Twenty
sixth avenue, near Point Lobos avenue,
| were referred to the Fire Department.
i After the meeting P. M. Gopcevic, a
member of the F. M. Yorke Company,
which has the contract for repairing
bituminous pavements, held a consul
tation with President Schmitz. The
latter urged Gopcevic to resume the
work of repairing the streets as the
Supervisors' Finance Committee had
requested him to do. Gopcevic de
murred, saying that his bills had not
been paid for August and September on
the pretext that the measurements of
the area covered had not been correctly
made.
Gopcevic, after consulting with F. M.
Yorke, flatly refused to go on with the
work- He claims that the Finance
Committee has acted in bad faith by
holding up the bills for work already
done; also that the company's contract
for paving H street was abrogated be
cause the Supervisors had not recom
mended the Improvement, although the
contract for improving Nineteenth ave
nue, which was also not recommended,
had been settled.
The Board of Works will report the
matter to the Supervisors' Finance
Committee and it is likeiy that steps
will be taken to forfeit the contract
and do the work either by inviting new
bids or by day labor by the city itself.
TURCHETTI CHARGED WITH MURDER.
In the Inquest on the body of William Mc-
Allister yesterday a Coroner's Jury returned
a verdict charging Alvlso Turchetti with mur
der. Turchetti shot and killed McAllister on
Pacific street on Christmas night.
AMATEUR CLUBS
ARE UNDER FIRE
The Athletic Officials Are
Finally Moving Against
Small Fight Promoters
3JORE WEIGHT FOR GANS
Baltimore Vaudeville Artist
and Gardner Will Not
Meet at Lightweight Limit
If the Supervisors are sincere in
their stand that they will suppress the
so-called amateur boxing clubs if evi
dence is forthcoming to show they are
sailing under false colors there may
ba no more such exhibitions.
At the annual meeting of the Pacific
Athletic Association the governing
body of amateur sport on this coast,
President William Greer Harrison of
the Olympic Club made a strong ad
dress dealing chiefly with this evil.
He said he, individually, would render
all the assistance in his power to
stamp It out and would guarantee the
aid of the Olympic Club.
The matter was brought forcibly
home to the Olympic Club last Friday
night when Sam Berger, one of its
members, took part in one of these af
fairs. The directors have sufficient
evidence before them to warrant drop
ping Berger from the membership
roll. When he was elected a regular
member it was with the understanding
that he would hand the secretary his
written resignation. This was to be
used at the option of the directors.
Jimmy Britt presented the same
form of resignation when he was
elected. In his case he is an avowed
professional, while Berger is charged
with sailing under false colors. The
directors will act on Berger's case to
morrow night.
The fight promoting clubs have been
making big money out of the young
boys who make up the major portion
of their cards. The receipts at three
of these affairs recently were respec
tively $2900, $3200 and $3600. The
boys are paid a mere pittance, while
the managers pocket big profits.
Although It was given out on Tues
day that Gans and Jimmy Gardner
were to meet here at 133 pounds at
6 o'clock, these figures were amended
yesterday to read 136 at 6 o'clock.
It is known Gans would have difficulty
In touching the lightweight limit.
Gardner is said to be no better off, as
h.. la a big, rangy young fellow, who
is growing rapidly.
After Gans' poor showing with Britt
the promoters are taking a chance on
tho attitude the public will assume
when he .appears again. There will
surely be little betting until Herford
declares with which man he wants to
win. Possibly they should be coupled
in the betting. His friends openly
boasted after the Britt fight that it
was originally Intended that Jimmy
should win, but the Baltimore manipu
lator thought there would be more
money in the betting to have Gans win
and Gans did win.
After the "fight" Gans told one man
he could have beaten Britt in one
round, while to another he confided he
could have beaten him with one hand
tied behind him. Even a nearsighted
man can see Gardner's finish if the
Baltimore flreater Is sent in to fight
and not to tfake. The public which
patronizes these exhibitions will have
to take its chances as to what it will
see.
LEARN SCHOOLS' NEEDS
FOR SANITARY REPAIRS
Supervisor d'Ancona and Director
Roncovierl Visit Several Build
ings to Take Notes.
School Director Roncovieri and Su
pervisor d'Ancona yesterday visited
several schools for the purpose of as
certaining: their needs in the matter of
sanitary improvements and repairs.
This is one of several visits already
made by the city officials named, it
being: their desire to acquaint them
selves with the actual requirements to
put the school buildings in proper
shape at once in order that the Fin
ance Committee may allow some
money for the same.
Superintendent of Schools Langdon
yesterday issued a circular of Instruc
tion to principals and teachers. All
day school principals are requested to
meet the Superintendent at room 356
City Hall on Monday, January 9, at
3:45 p. m.
On the opening of the spring term
the B fifth grade will continue the
Eggleston history and "Wood Folk at
School"; the A fifth will use the "New
State Introductory History," but not
"Wood Folk at School." Use the
history for both reading and study.
One Denman medal and one Bridge
medal will be allowed 80 each school
entitled to the same. Principals are
requested to send for order for the
same at once.
A musical teacher will be sent as
a representative from each school to
confer with the supervisor of music
in reference to the coming Mayday
festival. These teachers will meet in
room 7 of the Lincoln School on
Wednesday. January 25, at 4:15 p. m.
The California Club announces that
Jacob Rils will lecture at the Alham
bra Theater Monday evening. Janu
ary 9; subject, "Tony's Hardships."

TRACKS OFFER
CORRIGAN AID
Officials Join the Racing
Magnate iv His Fight
With Western Jockey Club
NEWPORT TURNED DOWN
Famous Outlaw Course Will
Not Be Included in the
New Circuit Now Forming
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CHICAGO, Jan. 4.âEdward Corrigan
and his forces met in Mr. Corrigan's
room at the Annex to-day and dis
cussed their plans of warfare in the
coming battle with the Western Jockey
Club. During the day they were vis
ited by a representative of the famous
Newport, Ky., track, and were in re
ceipt of a telegram from P. J. Car
mody of St. Louis, stating the St. Louis
courts had set aside the recent sale of
Union track and offering his support in
case he and his associates regained con
trol of the course.
The significance attached to this de
sire on the part of outside racing asso
ciations to assist Corrigan, Devereaux,
Winn, Respess and others in the fight
tends to show that sympathy may plsy
a part in the coming clash of the West
ern Jockey Club tracks and the new
circuit that will undeniably be formed
to oppose them.
During the conference, which lasted
all day and well into the evening, Mr.
Nolan of Cincinnati made his appear
ance in the interest of the famous out
law Newport track, last conducted by
John J. Ryan of "get-rich-qulck" no
toriety.
Mr. Nolan addressed Mr. Corrigan in
person and immediately began to in
quire as to the prospects of the New
port track being taken into the new cir
sult among others. Corrigan told No
lan he wanted nothing to do with New
port at any stage of the game, and
those present voiced his sentiments.
Nolan persisted, but his attempt for
recognition was futile and he went
away discouraged.
Corrigan and the men who are fight
ing for the rights with him refrained
from divulging their immediate plans.
"PITTSBURG PHIL" IS ILL.
Famous Plunger in Serious Condition
in a North Carolina Sanitarium.
NEW YORK, Jan. 4.âGeorge E.
Smith, "Pittsburg Phil," it was report
ed along Broadway this afternoon, is
seriously ill in Asheville, N. C, where
he has been for sSncie time in a sani
tarium. Mr. Smith has been in ill
health for several years. Last sea
son he was reported dying in the Adi
rondacks. He was somewhat con
spicuous around Eastern race courses
for a portion of the season, although
his operations in the ring were by no
means so extensive as formerly.
During the holidays he sent many
Christmas presents to old friends In
Pittsburg, where he first began his
remarkably successful career as a
backer of race horses. It is said
"Phil" still continues to take an inter
eat in the current races and that he
has had some success In picking win
ners at Los Angeles and New Orleans.

Schooner Ida flcKay Dismasted as She Reaches Port
The schooner O. M. Kellogg, which
arrived yesterday morning from Rara
tonga* was run into Tuesday niglit
while lying at anchor off the heads
by the schooner Ida McKay, bound in
from Eureka. The Kellogg escaped
'Without injury, but the Ida McKay
â¢looked very much the worse for wear
when she came into port. Her rigging
â¢oft the starboard side was carried away,
her- mizzen mast was lying on deck in a
tangle of wire rope and the shifting
rÂ»f her deckload gave her a heavy list.
Bhe strained her timbers against the
Btout sides of the Kellogg and had
t,wo feet of water In her hold when
she anchored. Nobody was injured on
either vessel.
The O. M. Kellogg. Captain Iversen,
is from Raratonga. a small Island of
the Cook group. The harbor at Rara
tonga was just wide enough to admit
the Kellogg, with about two feet to
spare on either side. The Island itself
â¢is only six miles long by four miles
wide, but is exceedingly fruitful. Cap
tain Iversen says the place is the near
esl to Paradise he ever expects to find
on this earth. The fifty whites and
400 natives that make up Raratonga's
population are all most hospitably in
clined and when Captain Iversen was
r.nt being entertained ashore as many
of the inhabitants as could crowd on
beard were dancing on the Kellogg's
deck.
"It is Paradise unspoiled," said Cap
tain Iversen yesterday, "and long may
it be kept from the kind of civiliza
tion that has spoiled many of the
South Sea islands. The people are
kind and intelligent, healthy, easy-go
ing and prosperous. They have a reli
gious service once every two months
and the merchant is a "bigger citizen
than the missionary. I'm a pretty
ancient mariner, but of all the places I
*>ver visited Rrtrntonga holds first
rlace. If I'd discovered it earlier I'd
have been one of its leading citizens
now."
Raratonga ships about 150 tons a
month of tropical products, mostly to
Australia. The Kellogg brought 392
ttins of copra and 46.000 cocoanuts.
Raratonga is about "00 miles from
Tahiti and the have much in
common. Tahiti having the advantage
o? regular communication with the
outside world.
Tug Sea Rover Returns.
The tug Sea Rover, which went north to
assist the disabled steamer Lakme to port, re
turned yesterday, having failed to find the
broken down coaster. The Lakme, in tow of
tne North Fork, was driven by the eoutherly
.'itorm far to the north of Humboldt. This.
.r""cbably I? the reason the Rover's mission
waÂ« a failure. WhÂ»n last seen the North
was still struggling with her water
logged tow.
Abydos Coaling in Stream.
The schooner E. B. Jackson, which arrived
-her 23 from Newcastle, Australia, with
ll'X) tons cf coal, is transferring her cargo to
the bunkers of the Kosmos liner Abydos, which
Is at anchor off Harrison street. So many
Kcsr-TD boats have arrived of late that some
6 * ilty has been experienced in eecurins
suitable wharf space for the loading and dis
charging of cargo.

NOTEâIn the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the

the charts. The plane cf reference Is the mean
of the lower low waters.
Brier Holme Uninsurable.
The British bark Brier Holme, which has
been on the overdue list for some time, yes
terday was declared uninsurable. She is out
167 days from London for Hobart and was
quoted at 90 per cent. The rate on the Stork
has been advanced to 70 per cent and the
Edith Mary to 85 per cent.
Was Seized by Japanese.
The British tramp steamer Crusader, which
arrived Tuesday night from Moji with 6000
tons of coal, was captured by the Japanese
on her voyage from Puget Pound to Shanghai.
She was taken to Hakodate, but released as
soon ss the Japanese were satisfied that her
destination was a neutral port.
Courser Raised and Righted.
The coal barge Courser, which played the
star part in the recent southeaster. was> righted
yesterday and in a few weeks will be repaired
and in commission aÂ»ain. She capsized oft
Harrison-street wharf, and three men who
composed her crew narrowly escaped drowning.

ber as dunnange valued at $390.
Crude Oil for the North.
The tank steamer Argyll, which was cleared
yesterday for Portland via Vancouver, car
ried 10.000 barrels of crude oil in bulk, valued
at $8000. for Vancouver, and 15,086 barrels
for Portland.

Surrendered by His Mother.
John Fraser, the boy for whom the
Mission police have been looking and
who is said to have been one of the
party which held up and robbed a
young girl on the Corbett road a few
days ago, was brought before Captain
M. O. Anderson of the Mission sta
tion yesterday afternoon by his
mother. The boy is only 10 years of
age and had been in hiding in dif
ferent parts of the city since the day
on which it is alleged he took part
in the affair. He had been sleeping in
sheds and barns until driven home by
cold and hunger. As soon as he came
home and got something to eat his
mother escorted him to the station and
surrendered him.

Arthur Loeb pleaded guilty In Judge Dunne's
court yesterday to a charge of felony embez
zlement and was ordered to appear for sen
tence on January 10. He was employed by
Flelschmann A Co.. distillers. 508 Front street
and on August 12 he collected $190, which he
appropriated to hli own use.

HOPS AND MALT
CAUSE STRIFE
Stockholders of a Brewing
Company Have a Quarrel
Over Division of Profit
OLD REVOLVER DRAWN
August Meyer Swears Out a
Warrant for the Arrest
of Partner and Employer
If the saloon men who purchase
i foamy steam and lager from the Col
j umbia Brewing Company discover
blood stains all over their beer kegs
I they need not be astonished, for there
are activities not induced by hops in
| the joy factory at 326 Clementina street.
> Tuesday evening the hard feelings of
. the two owners of the establishment
i found vent in a vociferous quarrel, and
' before the dispute was ended President
and General Manager H. D. Ulrichs
flourished a revolver in the face of
: August Meyer, a stockholder and
! driver.
| Yesterday Meyer swore out a war
'â  rant for Ulricas' arrest, charging that
j the books of the concern were kept
! from him and that he had been
| assa ted. - ach says the other is try
' ing to get control of the entire estab
; lishment and both punctuate their as
! sertions with remarkably forceful
; German-Engiish epithets,
i The Ulrichs family controls one-half
jof the capital stock. The Meyer family
controls the other half. The president
of the concern, however, controls all
the directors. When a stockholders'
meeting is called, Meyer is present with
half but less than a majority of the
stock. Ulrichs fails to vote and the
directors remain unchanged. This
might suit Meyer were it not for the
fact that the directors handle the
money.
Ulrichs says, "* gave him half of the
profits."
Meyer says. "He takes half the
profits, I get half and his son gets
another half."
This form of division is under the
scrutiny of the courts as a result of
ihe filing of five different civil suits.
Meyer says he simply wanted to get a
view of the books Tuesday evening.
Ulrichs says his fellow-etockholder
wished to destroy the corporation's rec
ords. At any rate, Ulrichs drew a
weapon that must have been used by
the Chinese when they first invented
gunpowder 500 years before the Chris
tian era and Meyer dropped the books.
After further fracturing the already
battered atmosphere. Meyer went home.
Ulrichs was released on $40 cash bail.
A young Ulrichs is secretary and
wagon driver for the concern. The case
will be discussed in the Police Court to
morrow.
INQUIRES INTO
EPPINGER CASE
The Grand Jury yesterday turned its
attention to the alleged swindle that
Eppinger & Company perpetrated on
the banks by means of forged ware
house certificates. A number of wit
nesses were examined, but no definite
conclusion was reached in the effort to
bring about more effective prosecution.
Jacob Eppinger, one member of the
grain firm, was tried some months ago,
but the jury disagreed. His brother
Herman has also been charged, and
the trial is set for the near future.
Colonel J. C. Beck, who was for
merly manager \of the International
Banking Company, the principal suf
ferer from the looting of the ware
houses, was called to testify yester
day. President Bowles of the .Ameri
can Natio:.al Bank was also a witness.
The others called were C. F. Herlitz
and Robert Williams. The latter came
from Crockett. Contra Costa County,
where Eppinger & Company had a
big warehouse. He was a grain in
spector at the time the frauds were
committed.
The Jurors also investigated the case
of B. C. Parks, who was tried in Judge
Lawlor's court on a charge of em
bezzlement, escaping conviction by a
disagreement of the jury. It is alleged
that while serving as manager of a
men s furnishing store on Sixth street,
owned by Mrs. Mary Selowsky, he mis
appropriated funds to the amount of
$9000. Parks disappeared from the city
after his trial, but he has returned and
It is desired ro have him indicted. No
indictment was returned yesterday, but
it probably will be to-morrow night, to
which time the Grand Jury adjourned.
The Police Committee, which has
been investigating corruption in China
town, reported progress. Not much has
been accomplished for several days.
WORK DELAYED
BY ONE TENANT
The construction of a twelve-story
building by the Humboldt Savings
Bank at 18 Geary street may be tem
porarily delayed by legal proceedings.
It was proposed to begin work imme
diately, but one of the tenants of the
structure now on the premises refuses
to move and a suit for eviction will be
commenced by the bank to-day.
The building now on the premises is
a three-story structure and the base
ment is occupied by a saloon known as
"Faust Hall.'* This saloon was leased
one year ago from the bank by the
San Francisco Breweries (Ltd.) and it
is over the terms of the lease that the
dispute has arisen. The lease calls for
three years and the brewery people re
fuse to move until that time has ex
pired. The banking people admit that
the lease was written for three years,
but they also aver that a specification
was made that the tenants could be
evicted at the end of one year should
they desire to improve the property.
Soon after the lease was made the
bank made preparation for the erection
of a twelve-story office building and
notified the tenants that they would
have to move by the first of the year.
This request met with no objection
except from the brewing company and
the building, with the exception of the
basement, was unoccupied and ready
for destruction before December L
The bank, which had been doing busi
ness on the first floor for the past thir
ty-five years, moved to 626 Market
street and the other tenants scattered
elsewhere. But the holders of the
lease for "Faust Hall" remain obdu
rate and the legal contest will begin
with the suit to evict them, which will
be brought this morning.
Attorney William Barnes for Bauer
RUBBER STOCK
SALE OPPOSED
Application of West Coasi
Company's Assignee Is De
feated by Chief Creditor
FREDRICKS IS OBJECTOR
Former President Says That
He Has No Confidence in
the Man Now in Control
The application of Don A. Sutherland,
assignee of the West Coast Rubber
Company, for permission to sell the
$40,000 stock of the concern in parcels
at private sale did not meet with Judge
Troutt'B approbation yesterday and,
though stating that he would not make
a final decision until the opposing sides
had been given a chance to reach an
agreement, the court said that the ap
plication was altogether too broad.
Attorney T. C. Van Ness, represent
ing George Fredrlcks. former presi
dent of the company and its heaviest
creditor, opposed the application and
demanded that no sale should be made
until Fredrlcks had been notified and
given a chance to object. He said that
Fredricks had no confidence in the as
signee nor in the persons who procured
his appointment, chiefly Bennett, Good
all & Co. He even declared that Suth
erland was incompetent to handle the
stock satisfactorily, and intimated that
through the medium of a third party a
sale of the goods might be effected at a
price below its real value, to the-detri
ment of Fredricks, who, besides having
the largest claim, was also in the posi
tion of a deficiency debtor.
Attorney Kellogg, representing the
assignee, took exceptions to Mr. Van
Ness' statements and declared that the
assignee was under heavy bonds and
was entitled to some confidence. He
declared further that Fredricks was not
entitled to as much consideration as
the other creditors because he had
started a suit against the company
which had precipitated the insolvency
proceedings.
Judge Troutt was of the opinion that
there should be some check put upon
the assignee in the disposition of the
stock, and that the objecting creditor
was entitled to protection. He advised
the attorneys for each side to get to
gether and agre: upon an order of sale
to be submitted to him later.
Tivoli Seat Sales Begin.
The sale of reserved seats for the
first week of the Tivoli season of
grand opera will open at the box office
of the theater at 9 o'clock this morn
ing. The prices will range from $2 to
50 cents. The entire company is now
en route from the City of Mexico and
will reach San Francisco in time to
hold a series of rehearsals on the
stage of the Tivoli before the opening
night, which is definitely set for
Wednesday evening, January 11. The
opening opera will be Verdi's "Rigo
letto," which will serve to introduce
Mme. Tettrazzini. It will be followed
by either "Zaza" or "La Tosca," in
which Mme. Berlindi will make her
debut. "The Barber of Seville" and
"Andre Chenier" will complete the
cycle of the first week's perform
ances. The season promises to be a
notable one in San Francisco's grand
opera annals. The Tivoli's great stage
is being put into condition to receive
the invading forces of grand opera
and the very atmosphere of the the
ater is surcharged with music.

MRS. MONNIER
MAKES DENIAL
Says That Answers She Gave
in Annulment Suit Were
Unintentionally Wrong
GALLAND ON THE STAND
Swears Addition of Name to
Adoption Paper Was Owing
to Lack of Verification
Sullivan and Roche, the attorneys for
George Monnier, who is suing hia
mother for a share in the estate of his
father, the deceased wine merchant,
had their first opportunity to cross-ex
amine Mrs. Monnier Sr. yesterday, and
during the morning session in Judge
Graham's court elicited several denials
from her as to testimony given at tha
time of young Monnier's annulment
suit.
The break In Mrs. Monnier's testi
mony waa caused by the placing of
Jesse Galland, a deputy sheriff, on the
stand. Galland was studying law at
the time of George Monnier's adoption
and drew up the adoption papers.
Efforts were made by Sullivan and
Roche to cast suspicion on the regu
larity of the signing of the document,
but Galland was able to show that he
withheld the mother's signature from
the paper until he could verify the
spelling of the name. His cross-exam
ination will be continued this morning.
Mrs. Monnier spent a greater part of
the morning, under George D. Collins*
questions, denying the statements mad*
by the witnesses for the contestant as
to her use of vulgar language and ncr
treatment of George, his wife and
children. She said that she had shown
no partiality to either of George's
children, treated them both alike, and
often bought them clothing.
On the completion of Collins' examin
ation, Sullivan took the witness in
hand and attempted to shake her tes
timony as to the questions she an
swered In the suit brought by George
Monnier Jr. for the annulment of his
marriage with Violet. At that time she
alleged that George always had
stomach trouble and that she had
never seen him drunk.
During the present trial she testified
to the contrary and yesterday told
Sullivan that George had stomach
trouble only at intervals and that
when she said she had never seen him
drunk she meant at night time. She
said that she had often seen him in
toxicated during the day. The case will
be continued this morning at 10 o'clock.

THURSDAY. â¢ . ... . JANUARY 5. 1905
THE AFFAIRS OF STATE.
GOVERNOR PARDEE'S excellent message is a business doc- j
ument of great interest to all citizens. It is a disclosure of the |
rapid growth of California and the corresponding expansion
of its public business. The enlightened policy compelled by Governor j
Pardee two years ago has put the State's finances again in good
shape and has overcome the mistaken policy of making the tax levy
less than the appropriations.
To correct this error of the Legislature which preceded the
last has required a large levy and an increase in taxation, but this <
came at a time when the general prosperity of the people bore the |
exaction without appreciable injury. During the last two years the j
State has expended in betterments to the public property the large
sum of $793,943 46. As these betterments were mostly enlargements :
of facilities to accommodate the increasing number of objects of the
State's care, they show a notable increase in our population, out of I
which issue the "classes that must have care or restraint, as well as
that more significant and valuable class that must be provided with
the means of education.
The large amount of tax collections for State revenue, now by |
law compelled to be kept in the State vaults, is a perceptible with- j
drawal of money from circulation and affects business unfavorably
by causing a stringency in the money market. The Governor wisely 1
and forcibly recommends that this policy of hoarding be abandoned
and that the State funds be returned at once to circulation by de- 1
positing them in banks at interest and on proper security. He says
that the amounts now hoarded uselessly to the State and injuriously
to business run from $2,000,000 to $7,000,000, and have averaged for
the last five years $4,000,000. This average at 2 per cent interest j
would return to the State the large sum of $80,000 annual interest, j
That sum would support one of the State institutions, or it might |
be added as an annual accretion to the endowment of our very needy j
State University. The Governor admits that an amendment to the
constitution is necessary to effect this most necessary reform, but
he makes it plain and convincing that whatever measure is necessary
should be taken, and at once, by this Legislature.
Due to the efforts of The Call the last Legislature changed the j
methods of investment of the school funds of the State. The ex- ;
pensive intermediation of brokers was abolished and the Board of
Examiners were empowered to bid directly for bonds, without show
ing a certified check. The message says the effect is noticeable in a 1
closer and better investment of the school fund and an increase in
the rate of interest. The Governor strongly supports the State
militia and deprecates the policy of certain citizens and organizations
that oppose it and seek to put industrial disabilities upon members
of the National Guard.
The message strongly supports our common school system and i
points to the gratifying fact that about one-half of the State revenue
goes to its support. This is something that should appeal to the
pride of all Californians. Education is a restraint upon crime and
misfortune, and the more general it becomes the less will be the cost
of the punitive and eleemosynary institutions. Money spent in the
schools is the wisest of public economy, and the whole State will j
appreciate and remember the Governor's good words for this great- j
est of American institutions.
The State will be glad to see that a considerable portion of the :
message is occupied by a most clear and convincing statement of the
need of tax reform. This is in line with Governor Pardee's inaugural |
and with the policy advocated by the State Grange and supported j
by The Call. The message proposes separation of the assessment
and taxation for support of the State government from the system j
of local revenue entirely. The history of taxation in this State ; s
traced through the old constitution and court decisions, and through
the debates in the convention of 1879. The Governor quotes the
late Samuel M. Wilson, a delegate to the convention that made the
present fundamental law. Opposing the rigidity of the present con
stitutional requirement. Mr. Wilson said: "My individual opinion
is that the constitution should only prescribe that all real estate and
all tangible property should be taxed, and that such taxation shall
be according to value, and that the Legislature shall have, tjeyond
that, the power and discretion of imposing any other tax it may
see fit except a capitation tax. Then the Legislature, in its discre- .
tion, could exact a stamp tax. a tax upon incomes, or a tax upon
anything else, according to the necessities of the State at the time.
That would bÂ£ justice to all and would be more elasticâin a betteÂ«"
condition and more in accordance with the general principles in all
the States of the Union. Jt is not necessary to equal and uniform
taxation that it should be universal."'
The Governor regrets that this wise advice was not taken by 'â¢
the convention, and expresses the general belief now existing, that
the time has come to follow it. and by a more elastic system permit
the State to derive its revenues by indirect taxation, selecting there
for those objects that are not subject to local levy. The makers of the
present constitution sought especially to increase the taxation of
personal prpperty. and therefore indulged in specific definitions of
personal property. B, ut the message says that the results have .
proved disappointing. The last year under the old constitution, the !
personal property taxed was 20 per cent of the whole. It has now
permanently declined to 15 per cent of the whole assessed valuation
of the State.
The elusive capacity of that form of property has enabled !
evasion of the purpose of the constitution. There must beWeform,
and now. By turning over to the State for 'its sole taxation the
forms in which personal property is corporately manifest, and sup- j
porting the State government whoJly by taxation of franchises, li- |
censes and other privileged uses of personal property, each county
and municipality will control its own levy, and not be subject to the
equalization of its assessment to meet the necessities of the State.
The Governor has made the subject and the necessity so clear that
the Legislature cannot well avoid taking action now.
The message is throughout a most satisfactory statement of the
condition of the public interests of the State, and puts the Legisla- j
ture under trustworthy guidance and advice.
THE CALL has nothing to add to its insistence that the United
States Senator should be accorded to Southern California. Every
reason derived from natural politics supports this view of the
matter, and no one has antagonized its propriety. The material
welfare and political peace of the State will be advanced by that
policy. It is right and it is expedient. To insist upon it detracts \
nothing from the candidates of the north. Their aspiration is hon- j
orable and their patriotism will ease the waiting period that must i
needs pass before its; realization. The Republican party is so strong j
in the Legislature and the State that it can afford to do right and be j
just. No party weak or strong can afford to do otherwise, but the j
party is subject to a peculiar call to duty, which we are persuaded it
will heed.
The geographical allotment of the office of Senator is not all
that it should do. California has submerged all past records under
its enormous majority for President Roosevelt. It did this not as f
the expression of a passing fancy, and not altogether as an assertion i
of party strength, and in it there is no idle meaning. California in-!
dorsed the President because the perJple believe in him and desire

will benefit from them as much as the people in whose behalf they
are to be made.

IN THE MAID OF
HONOR'S CARRIAGE
THE young man entered the car
riage, banged the door shut and
settled himself comfortably with
a sigh of relief. Opposite him
| sat a pensive young woman ln a be
! wildering gown of fluffy white. The
i young man removed his high hat and
mopped his perspiring brow. He ad
justed his tie and brushed the dust
; from his coat sleeve. The carriage
i rattled briskly over the pavements;
1 outside the early afternoon sunshine
| flooded the streets with a mellow glow.
; "Well," said the . young man,
i straightening himself and smiling at
; the girl, "another good man gone
! wrong. Every time I assist at the
j weddings of these reckless youths I
I feel somehow like an executioner."
| "We're getting to be arch-conspira
| tors in the wedding line, aren't we?"
I the girl laughed. "It seems of late
j that we are thrown together at every
1 one.
"Let's see," he mused, "this is the
third so far this month. We're ac
cumulating a great weight of responsi
i bility."
| "All my friends seem to be going in
j for matrimony," she said.
"Mine, too," he returned cheerfully.
I "Hymen is an indiscriminate old
burglar. He gobbles 'em right and
j left. And when a man's married he's
!as good as lost to his friendsâat first,"
he added.
"But they all come back to the
| club and âand the old life, don't
| tbey?" she said testily.
"Most of them," he admitted.
I "That's the man of it," she said.
"Marriage is a lottery," he said,
lacking momentarily a more original
I " 'Pleasant the snaffles of courtship,
improving the manners and car
riage.' " she quoted.
\ " 'But the colt who is wise will re
frain from the terrible thornbit of

"There seems to be a great many
colts lacking wisdom," she remarked.
"And lots more willing to lack that
I commodity." he laughed.
"Doesn't a wedding ever impress

"Always," he replied. "Often heart
rending."
"Well, mine shan't be," she said,

THE DIVINE PASSION
MAY BURN OR BLESS
BY DOROTHY FENIMORE,
| HAVE been asked by a reader if I
I think that love causes mortals
I more pain than joy, and if it is
â  an unsatisfied longing, like the
dream of happiness.
Love, answer I, is like a light in the
darkness. It burns or it blesses.
The moths who rush into the candle
light find it a scorching flame. To the
shipwrecked man at sea a beacon
means salvation. To the student a light
at night is opportunity, and to the
world at large it is suggestive of home
and fireside happiness.
So, too, with love. Headlong folly in
love results in disaster. Yet love is
hope to the discouraged, faith to the
skeptical, and to the lonely heart it is
the golden key to home.
There are two ways of judging love
âby what men say of it. and by what
you yourself find to be true. Both
these ways are full of prejudice. Take
the latter, for instance. If you have
played the moth you know love as a
flame. If love came to you in the hour
of your despair, you reverence it as a
messenger from heaven. If it has
opened up for you a new world of
poetry and beautiful reality, you will
care for tt as thoughtfully as the wise
virgins of the scriptures tended their
precious lamps. If love meant for you
a sweet companionship, for which, in
the solitude of your secret life you
had eagerly sighed, then you are sure
President. During all of his term as the successor of President Mc-
Kinley he has made plain his purpose in seeking election to a full
term. He is not a ntan of concealments, and does nothing hidden.
This State approves of his purpose to destroy the artificial inequali
ties which are the outgrowth of our enormous progress in production,
commerce and transportation. Reasonable men who are a part of
this artificial incubus upon the individual see the necessity for its
abolition, and will hail it as relief from a burden. In this situation
California should follow her impressive indorsement of the President
by strengthening his hands in the Senate.
We make no insinuation and raise no outcry against any cor
poration, nor against the interests of any capital. We bring no
charge against any influence that may seem apparent in the Sena
torial contest at Sacramento. We are willing to accept all indica
tions that corporations, looking across the conservative prospect pre
sented by the President's views, to that chaos which lies beyond, are
willing to enter into that co-operation to which he invites them. But
we wish to very soberly remind the members of the Legislature that
their public and party duty requires of them the election of a Senator
who will support the President. What they do will go to judgment
upon that proposition. A house cannot stand divided against itself.
The people put the Republican party in power in this State that it
might make good as the powerful supporter of popular rights. If
the Legislature seem to fail to make good in the election of a Senator
no seer is required to foretell the result.
The geographical requirements of the situation, and the strength
ening of the President are consistent with each other. They can
be accomplished at one stroke, and when accomplished the Legisla
tive hand will have written "perpetuity" over the career of the party
as far as it can be inscribed upon anything human.
To do otherwise will be to reach a lame and pitiful conclusion.
To indorse the President at the polls and repudiate him in the Leg
islature will require no explanation. It will be self-explanatory. This
issue is alive in every Republican Legislature that has to elect a
Senator. So far there is in none of them a sign that the party will
throw itself out of line with the President. The party everywhere
seems to be girding itself to make the contest to which he has called
it. Scarcely a note of dissent is heard from the party press. All these
things have keyed up the expectation of the country. Let not Cali
fornia be the first to disappoint it. ,

Plight of English Bank Clerks.
The bank clerks of England are plan
ning the formation of a union in order
to secure an increase of wages. They
say the present wages virtually con
demn the clerks to "celibacy, lodgings
and sixpenny lunches" for life.

TO GREAT MEN
More Suggestions Are Made
to Guide in Choice of
.. Two Types for Statues
rOLEMAN AGAIN NAMED
Jirnipero Serra Has Strong
Hold Upon Favor of the
Active Workers in City
Prominent San Francisco men yes
terday furnished additional indica
tions of the drift of public opinion re
garding the selection of eminent Cali
fornians to be commemorated at the
National Capitol by statues of marble
and bronze, the State being entitled to
erect two such statues.
Spencer C. Buckbee said William
T. Coleman deserved well of the
btate when it was deciding whom to
honor. Coleman twice headed the
Vigilance Committee. He was a man
â¢ut broad views and great enterprise.
His courage was conspicuous and his
services were of inestimable value at
a time when resolute leadership was
indispensable. He represented a cer
tain era. Californians were particu
larly interested in perpetuating the
memories of deeds of men who have
lived in the State since the American
occupation. For this reason Mr.
Buckbee said that the selection should
include oniy men who have partici
pated in California affairs since the
date of that occupation. Mr. Buck
bee's second choice was the Rev.
Thomas Starr King.
.. . Oscar F. Sites, manager of the
Palace Hardware Company, strongly
.favored giving a statue to Padre Ju
nipero Serra, whom he estimated to
have been a very great man and po
tential in the history of California.
â¢Mr. Sites also said William T. Cole
man was deserving of fame.
W. H. Mills said that Collis P. Hun
tington did more for California in a
material, way than any other man, and
he therefore favored Huntington.
Professor Joseph Le Conte, Mr. Mills
said, was better known to the world
.at large, by reason of his scientific
achievements, than any other Califor
nian. It was due to Huntington that
the first transcontinental railway was
'constructed. He raised the money.
It followed as a consequence that the
United States should in time become
a larsre factor in the commerce of
the Orient. Huntington's great ser
vice to California was that he merged
the State with the other parts of the
country. Before the railway was
built California was principally a
mining State. Wheat was exported,
but other products were grown prin
ipally to supply the mines. The rail
way alone opened up a way to reach
markets. Huntington and Le Conte
were Mr. Mills' selections.
â¢Vttorney William Thomas named
Padre Junipero Serra and the Rev.
Thomas Starr King, and said that
were both representative of im
portant periods and connected with
important happenings in the history
of California. He favored them as
subjects for statues.
NORTH SHORE ENJOINS
WORK OF ITS COMPETITOR
New Petaluma Road Restrained From
Ruilding Crossings Over Track
Pending Trial of Stut.
A temporary order was obtained
from Judge Seawell yesterday by the
California and Northwestern Railway
snd San Francisco and North Shore
Railway companies restraining the
Petaluma and Santa Rosa Company
from forcibly building one or more
crossings over the plaintiffs' tracks
in Sonoma County. It is related that
the defendant company has com
menced condemnation proceedings
to legally secure crossings for
its roads and this suit is now
pending, but for several weeks, by
use of threats and exhibition of force,
the defendant company has sought to
build the crossings without permis
sion and will succeed unless re
strained. An order to show cause why
the injunction should not be granted
was made returnable January 20.

SALAMMBO WILL
BE BEAUTIFUL
AT COLUMBIA
CLEVER APTEESS WHO WILL, AP
PEAR NEXT WEEK AT THE
COLUMBIA IN - SALAMMBO."
Among the more elaborate produc
tions of the present season will be
"Salammbo," which will be presented
here at the Columbia Theater on Mon
day evening by Frederick Warde and
Kathryn Kidder. From several stand
points it promises to be one of the
mtst interesting events of the present
season. Its stage pictures and cos
tumes will represent scenes and man
ners of dress absolutely unfamiliar and
the dramatic action will be accom
panied by a melodious musical score,
composed by Henry K. Hadley, neces
sitating an augmented orchestra. The
play Is laid in ancient Carthage at a
time when the proud city was harassed
by threats of Rome on one nand and
the rebellion of its own mercenary
armies on the other.
Owing to the engagement being
limited to one week only, there will be
matinees on Wednesday and Saturday
of next week.
COURT DECIDES
AGAINST SCOTT
The final chapter of the once noted
Sheehan-Scott case closed yesterday,
when the Supreme Court decided that
the plaintiff, Edward L Sheehan, was
entitled to the office of Tax Collector
of this city and county. But as the
term of office has expired more than
three years ago, Sheehan will receive
no other satisfaction than that of se
curing a Judgment over Scott.
J. H. Scott was elected Tax Collec
tor on November 7, 1599. Sheehan held
the office and claimed Scott had no
right to it. Sheehan made an attempt
to keep Scott from entering upon his
duties. He tried every means and all
but resorted to violence, in the mean
time bringing suit in the Superior
Court.
Sheehan alleged that Scott was not
entitled to the office on the ground
that he had not been a resident of the
city and county for a period of five
years as provided in the charter. Scott
fought the case bitterly and many
months after the suit was brought
judgment was rendered in favor of
Sheehan by Superior Judge Seawell.
In the meantime. Scott managed to
hold the office, although Sheehan did
everything possible to oust him. When
Judge Seawell decided in Sheehan's
favor, Scott appealed the case to the
Supreme Court. By this time the
office had nearly expired, but through
influence and technicalities Scott still
retained his position.
Now the Supreme Court comes forth
and sustains the verdict handed down
by Judge Seawell. In the meantime
two other Tax Collectors have held
office since and both Sheehan and
Scott are practically dead politi
cally. Scott was elected to the office,
served out his term and drew down
his salary regularly. Sheehan con
tested the case, got a verdict in two
courts, but never drew a cent of salary
nor had the pleasure of ousting his
opponent.
Inasmuch as the Supreme Court haa
decided that a man who does the work
gets the salary. Sheehan is not entitled
to anything under the law. Still, two
; courts have ruled that the office is
, rightfully his, but now that other
i parties are in control and other elec
tions have been held, Sheehan must
be satisfied with the honor of securing
| two Judicial victories.
Claimants for Bail Money.
John J. Barrett, in a complaint in
Interpleader, has asked the court to
determine the ownership of a portion
of the ball money put up for the re
lease of Edwin E. Stoddard before he
was sentenced to eighteen months'
imprisonment for embezzlement. Of
the $1000 W. F. Hunt put up $600, F.
D. Spaulding $200 and Joseph Seeley
$300. Barrett has paid out $460 on
order of the owners and retains $$50
belonging to Hunt and $200 belonging
to Spaulding. which the depositors
have demanded, but the paymnt was
stopped by P. B. Bekeart on an at
tachment for a claim against Stod
dard. Barrett has no Interest in the
money and wants to deposit it ln
court.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, * JANUARY 5, 1905.
LOWE'S MEMORY
IS DEFECTIVE
Witness in Dunsmuir Case
Cannot Remember WTien
Questioned by Judge Coyne
FILE NOTICE OF MOTION
Mrs. Hopper's Attorneys, in
Formal Document, Bring
Case to Superior Court
"I don't remember" waa the answer
given to nearly every question of im
portance put to J. A. 8. Lowe by Judge
j Coyne during the taking of testimony
;in the Dunsmuir case yesterday morn
ing. On some points the witness
j would answer positively "Yes" or
! "No," but on the majority of questions
! put to him his mind was a blank.
James Peter Taylor, also a witness
to the will, was before the commission
; yesterday. His testimony was greatly
!at variance with that given by Lowe
lon Tuesday. A mild sensation was
sprung when Sir Charles H. Tupper pro
j duced before the commission a floor
plan diagram of the Agnew residence
| ln Oakland, where the will was signed.
Lowe was the first witness called
i yesterday. Judge Coyne took him in
; hand and put up a vigorous cross-ex
! amlnation. Lowe was non-committal.
:He took refuge in the statement "I
, don't remember" to nearly every ques
i tion asked him. The question of com
; pensatlon for his services was brought
|up for bis trip from England here. He
, said he had not been promised any
: specific sum, but he thought $20 a day
would be what his services were worth,
and he thought c.:iÂ»* representatives of
James Dunsmuir would pay him this
amount.
LOWE'S MEMORY.
Judge Coyne asked Lowe concerning
a certain letter that he (Lowe) had
! shown to Mr. Avery on the Sausaiito
j boat last Saturday morning. Lowe
could not remember anything about the
letter. After a terrific hammering from
Judge Coyne, Lowe was compelled to
admit that he had the letter referred
to, but could not remember anything
more about it. He further admitted
that the letter was from his wife, but
he positively declined to give its con
tents. He also said that he could not
remember having discussed the ques
tion of seeing an attorney to coach him
in the case. Finally Judge Coyne, in a
j fit of desperation, said: "Is there any
; thing you do remember?" "Yes,"
j Lowe replied, "I remember that I had
breakfast this morning." "Now Vr.
'. Lowe," said Judge Coyne, "do you re
i member going to the office of Mr.
: Thorne this morning at 10 o'clock?"
"Yes," said Lowe.
"What happened there?" queried
Judge Coyne.
L "I don't remember," replied Lowe.
"Do you pretend to tell me, sitting
here under oath before this commis
sion, that you do not know what hap
pened in Mr. Thome's office this morn
ing only about twenty minutes ago?"
"I don't remember," was Lowe's re
ply.
! "And I suppose that this testimony
you are giving now is as true as all the
rest of the testimony you have given
before this commission?" asked Judge
Coyne. "Yes," replied Lowe. This
wound up the morning session.
James Peter Taylor, a prominent coal
merchant, testified to the same effect
as he did during the trial of the case In
Victoria, B. C.
Kis statements regarding the doings
and movements of James and Alexan
der Dunsmuir, Lowe and himself, on
the night of the signing of Alexander's
< will, were entirely different from the
, statements made by Lowe on Tues
day.
POINTS TO DIAGRAM.
Ti ylor pointed out with accuracy on
the diagram the position in the parlor
of the Agnew home where the docu
! ment was signed. He declined posi-
I lively and emphatically to make a
I mark on the diagram but he put his
j finger on the spot in question. In the
testimony of Lowe on Tuesday, from a
drawing made by himself, he indicated
an exact opposite position in the room.
Albert James Bain, the architect who
drew up the floor plan of the Agnew
home, testified to his visit to that place,
and went minutely into a description
of the premises.
The further hearing of testimony will
take place Saturday morning at 10:30.
Edna Wallace Hopper's attorneys,
Campbell, Metson & Campbell, yester
day formally liled the notice, which
they had served on <he attorneys for
James Dunsmuir of their intention to
move on January 6 for a revocation of
the judgment of May 9, 1900, admitting
the will of Alexander Dunsmuir to pro
bate and appointing James Dunsmuir
as executor. The motion is to be based
upon the grounds that the court did not
have jurisdiction of the suhject-matter
of the estate nor of the persons inter
ested. The affidavits of Sir Charles
Hibbert Tupper and Edward P. Coyne,
regarding the proceedings in British
Columbia, are attached to the notice.
The O'Connor-Moffatt Sale.
The big alteration sale inaugurated hy
i O'Connor, Moffatt & Co. incident to the
' enlargement of their store is proving an
immense success. This is the first sale
. of a special nature held by the firm stnee
its great fire sale in 1883. The premises
have been so crowded with bargain seek
ers since last Thursday that the services
of police officers have been called into
requisition to regulate the movements of
the crowds. Officers Douglass and Ma
loney have been specially detailed for
this work and their good nature and
efficiency have called for general com
mendation. Notwithstanding the diffi
culty in getting to the sales counters,
the great reductions made and the high
class of goods offered have proven a suf
ficient attraction to keep buyers on hand.

, the shock following the burning out of a fuse

ATTUBOATIOM, AT VBOKXVMT KROONL
AGED LITIGANTS
ARE IN COURT
Charles Main, Eighty-Seven
Years Old, Is Prosecuting
Case Against His Agent
TRANSACTION IN BONDS
Claim That T.R. Hayes With
held Securities Purchased
With Plaintiffs Money
A beginning was made in Judge Sea- ;
well's court yesterday in the trial of
the case of Charles Main against
Thomas R. Hayes, personally and aa
executor of the will of his son, C. E.
Hayes, to recover 600 shares of stock in
the Standard Portland Cement Com
pany. The plaintiff, a member of the
firm of Main & Winchester, is 87 years
old. THe defendant went into the
firm's employ fifty-one years ago. For
the last ten or twelve years of his
service he was Main's confidential
agent, privileged to make investments
for him without question.
According to the complaint, in Sep
tember, 1902, Hayes bought fifteen of
the cement company's bonda during
Main's absence in the East and upon
Main's return, at his request, bought
five more. In January of this year,
Main says, he discovered that Hayes
and his son had received and withheld
600 shares of the company's stock
which were given as a bonus with the
bonds. The stock is declared to be
worth $45.0f10.
Hayes was on the stand and remem
bered tbe transaction and admitted
that the stock was issued ln his name
or that of his son. but he did not reach
an explanation as to his claim of
ownership, which was set forth ln his
answer to the complaint. He was with
drawn as a witness in order that the
prosecution might lay a foundation for
questions along that line by proving
the issuance of the stock by the evi
dence of the secretary of the com
pany. Then the case was continued
until next Monday morning.
One of the contentions in the answer
of Hayes is that $5000 worth of the
bonds were purchased for Main's
granddaughter, with her money. Main
in his deposition denies this strenu-
OUSIy and says that she has not a far
thing's interest in the security, al
though he might have intended to give
her the bonds later. A letter to him
from Hayes showed that there was an
entry in a certain book stating that the
bonds belonged to Mrs. Crawford's ac
count. In regard to this, Mr. Main
makes an interesting explanation. He
says Hayes kept his books and handled
his private affairs without question.
Main had implicit confidence in
Hayes and relied upon his judgment.
A great many years ago. when Main s
granddaughters were little children, he
started a separate account for them
and from time to time added to it in
the way of money and securities. In
the case of Mrs. Crawford, he says, her
account had reached $50,000 when she
married and with this she bought a lot
and built a home in Washington, D. C.
The aged plaintiff says that when he
found that stock had been given as a
bonus with the bonds he wrote to
Hayes about it and the latter denied
that it had been so issued; this, says
Main, was a source of great surprise to
him. The defense maintains that when
the stock was issued it was not worth
more than $6000 and is now valued at
only half of what the plaintiff alleges it
to be.
WILL SUBMIT ESTIMATES
OF STREET WORK COST
City Engineer and Supervisors to Co
operate in Carrying Out of
Bonded Improvements.
The Supervisors' Street Committee
yesterday secured from City Engineer
Woodward an agreemtn to s-.;bm:'
estimates of cost of sewering Mission
street, from Second to East, ami
for widening and repaying the street
from East street to Ninth; also for
sewering Fourth street, from Howard
to Berry, and other improvements in
other districts provided for ln the bond
issue. It is the intention of the com
mittee to order street Improvements
most urgently needed, especially in the
downtown districts, bids for sewer
bonds amounting to $724,800 and
street bonds for $243,000 having been
invited.
The committee recommended that
i sidewalk widths on both sides of Lake
i street, westerly from First avenue, be ;
, reduced from nineteen .to fifteen feet, ;
i and that sidewalk width on the north- 1
j erly side of Lincoln street, between
j Jones and Taylor, be five feet and the
| southerly side thereof be fourteen feet.

MINERS RESCUE
ENTOMBED MAN
Strikes Old Fill With His
Pick and the Falling Dirt
Shuts Him in Small Niche
IMPRISONED FOR HOURS
Sufficient Air Enters Cave
to Sustain Life Until His
Companions Dig Him Out
REDDING, Jan. 4.âGeorge Garwood,
Justice of the Peace of French Gulch,
was burled alive for nine hours to
day. Garwood and two partners have
a lease on the Pick-up mine in the
French Gulch district. The Judge was
at work to-day running an upraise to
connect one tunnel with another, when
suddenly his pick struck an old fill and
the fine dirt poured down on him, luck
ily leaving him in a niche in the wall.
In a twinkling he was shut ln with
barely room enough to turn around.
When his partners learned of the acci
dent they rushed to the Washington
mine near by, where they got the en
tire force of twenty miners to help
them dig Garwood out. It took the
miners nine hours, working in five
minute shifts, to rescue the imprisoned
man. They found him all right, as
sufficient air reached him through the j
old fill to keep him alive, and the cave- j
in had not injured him.

celved applications to mine by hydraulic pro

mine, near Port Wine. Sierra County, Cal..
draining into Morrtstown Ravine, thence into
Yuba River; from Jerry Curtis, In Forest
Grove mine, near Johnsvllle, Plumas County,
Cal.. draining into East Nelson Creek, which
reaches Feather River; from North Star Min
ing Company, in Lanoe.ll Placer mine, near
Mokelumne Hill, Calaveras County, Cal.
draining Into Rich Gulch, which reaches
Calaveras River: and from J. A. Hartman.
In Clayton Ranch Ravine mine, near Forbes
town. Yuba County, Cel., draining Into Dry
Creek, which reaches Yuba River, gives no
tice that a meeting to receive any protests

CONDEMNED
MAN MAKES
CONFESSION
Lain OWil. Alias LUpton,
Who Is to Be Hanged Next
Friday, Assumes All Blame
for the Mathews Bobbery
TRIES TO ASSIST
TWO OTHEB FELONS
Says That Mike and Docia
Nolan, Who Are Undergo
ing Long Sentences for the
Crime, Are Both Innocent
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN RAFAEL, Jan. 4.âLarry O'Nell,
alias George Lupton, alias Henry Mil
ton, who is to be hanged Friday at
San Quentin, has made a confession,
ln which he tries to exonerate Mike
Nolan and Docia Nolan from any con
nection with the robbery of Mrs.
Mathews on March 4, 1903. in her home
in San Francisco, for which crime both
the Nolans are undergoing long terms
of imprisonment.- O'Nell handed to
Warden Tompkins to-day a closely
written document of three pages which
ho calls his "confession." It begins:
"1,. Henry Milton, being of sound mind
and knowing that I am auout to die,
do exonerate Mike and Docia Nolan
from any connection with the robbery
oÂ£ Mrs. Mathews." He then sets forth
that his first acquaintance with Mike
and Docia Nolan was after his incar
ceration in the County Jail for the
murder of James Gillice. He knew
neither of the Nolans before that. *He
says he entered the house of Mrs.
Mathews with two confederates, whose
names he does not give, because he
does not want to involve them. It was
O'Neil, according to tne confession,
that entered the house and locked the
door and not Nolan. O'Neil says noth
ing as to who gave Mrs. Mathetvs the
terrible beating or who made the de
mand upon her for her valuables. His
next statement is that he was the last
one to leave the house and jump over
the back fence. The rest of the state
ment is rather disconnected and bears
reference to certain features of the
crime without telling the story of the
crime in its natural order. Herbert
Popplewell, the messenger boy, he as
serts was correct in his statement that
he saw Nolan and spoke to him at the
corner of Haight and Fillmore streets,
but how Milton knows this he does not
state. He denounces Ruby Grill's story
of the conspiracy between Docia and
Mike Nolan as perjury and insists that
Mrs. Mathews was mistaken in her tes
timony that Nolan was in her house
and struck her. When No;an was on
his way to receive his sentence from
Judge Cook Milton says ne was then
ready md willing to confess, but his
cellmates told him if he did he would
surely be hanged so he kept silent.
Milton's offense, for which he has
been condemned to death, was the mur- \
der of James Gillice, an aged saloon- <
keeper, April 15. 1903. Gillice had closed
his saloon and was walking along
Ninth street, near Market, when Mil
ton held him up Gillice resisted the
attempt to rob him and Milton shot
him in the groin. Milton did not
escape, horVever, for Gililce held him
until the police arrived. Gillice died
the following day.
Captain of Detectives Burnett places
no confidence in the story told by
O'Neil for the reason that Nolan
pleaded guilty to the robbery of Mrs.
Matthews. Captain Burnett thinks the
confession is only a ruse to help Nolan
now that O'Neil knows there is no sal
vation for himself.

The Lumber Market.
. Pacific Coast Wood and Iron says of lum
'â¢'.ber:
"The year just closed has been a memor
able one for both redwood and the product
.â¢ of Oregon and Washington mills. While the
'.manufacturers of redwood have succeeded in
-matatainlng prices and making money, the
â .mills of the Northwest have not done as well.
They have had several depressing Influences.
First, there was an overproduction, the for
' elgn or offshore demand tailing to almost
."nothing; and second, the freight rate to the
Missouri River was almost prohibitive.
â¢At the close of 19C3 the prioe of Oregon
â¢ lumber was $10 20; It closed 1904 at $7 30
â¢ for rough, which was really below the cost
of manufacture. Many of the larger mills
â¢ wisely closed down, preferring to wait until
'a' demand springs up. which it is piedlcted
will come early In the season, aa It Is hoped
that, the large stocks in Australia and the
island,, of the Pacific will be worked off and
. orders- again flood the market.
â¢ "California has had a prosperous year and
. alf oVOf the State fair prieea for lumber
';have prevailed. At Los Angeles there has
'been a rather slow year. Large quantities
â¢of lumber have beon disposed of, but there j
has been a cut In prices that has naturally
resulted In a loss to the handlers of both
' nine and redwood.
"Th* mountain mills have done well and
on the whole have had a prosperous year.
..V great showing will be made .in mountain
pine In .1905. and if thcrailroada deal.Justly
'with- the millmen the East will receive
..a.'Rood supply, not only of redwood, but sugar
'{15.i yellow pine. Shingles have done well.
The new year opens well for all concerned and
. j>roiÂ»perts are very bright. ThÂ« lumbermen ot
nhe eras' have every reason to expect great
thine? of 1906."
