' â¢ Now York Stock Market.
NEW YORK, Jan. 4.âThere was a decided
" abatement of speculative Interest in stocks,
â  and a resulting heavy Irregular tone ln the
â¢day's dealings. To some extent this was un
doubtedly due to the fear that the advances
â¢ of yesterday were too fast a pace for the health
of. the market. There was a perceptible sen
sitiveness also to the constant fluctuations in
Amalgamated Copper, which continues to bo
.regarded as & danger point by a large element
among the operators since their experience a
few weeks ago. The threatening utterances
â¢from the same source as at the time of the
previous break in the stock keepe alive this
..spirit of apprehension. The stimulating ef
. feet of the Northern Securities decision had
lost some of Its force to-day, and Northern
' Securities on the curb and all the stocks re
lated to it on the exchange were reactionary.
It Is taken for granted that plans are far ad
vanced toward an agreement. But there is a
remaining force in the consideration that the
. terms fixed for the redistribution of the prop
erties In the merger will end the control se
cured by the Union Pacific of the old North
ern Pacific, thus in a measure relegating con
. dfti >ns to the same state as that existing be
fore the colossal contest between the mag
. nates of the railroad world In 1901. There
was an Inclination to depend more upon the
expected January Investment demand for secur-.
itles. Thia proved to be of moderate propor
tions and was met by offerings on the part -
of the dealers, who had taken stocks ln an
ticipation of this demand. There were sus
taining points in the market, however, which
held prices against any severe reaction. New
" York Central was notably strong and reports
persisted of a large Interest concerned In this
property by Union Pacific and Standard Oil
interests, which would tend to harmonise its
operation with other railroad properties. The
. "marked buying for London account was felt
as. yesterday. The offering of the Irish land I
purchase loan absorbed a large Interest in that
market, and preparatione for the Russian loan
â¢ o,i the Continent formed another deterrent in
;â  ftuence. The money market here continued
extremely easy, but the rate of the goH outgo i
â  was of some sentimental influence on the stock !
market. In addition to the week's export* to
Fiance and South America it waa intimated !
that gold was likely to be shipped from San ,
Francisco to Japan on account of deposits â 
at the New York Sub-Treasury. The only oc
. casion that can call for shipments to Japan
st this time would be on account of New York
subscriptions on the Japanese loan. It is in
â  terestlng to recall that at the time of the last
â  Russian loan It was alleged that its flotation
â¢ was facilitated by reason of disbursements In
, the world's markets of drafts on deposits in 1
â¢European banks of the proceeds of the former
â¢ Japanese loan. The calling borne by Japan
- ot Its foreign deposits would promise a con
trary effect. *
- v R alltoad net earnings reported for Novem
ber showed good increases, an important In
nance being Union Pacific. The reactionary
tendency in the market, however, prevailed,
find the close was weak.
so NÂ°nds were easy. Total sales, par ralue,
Pnited States bonds were unchanged on call.

haustion of winter wheat. Kansas City and St-
Louis to-day only receiving 91.000 bushels.

half what they were a year ago. The princi
pal factor, however in the late situation was a
decrease of 2,561,000 bushels in the world's
available stocks. The market closed at prac
tically the highest point of the day. Final
quotations on May were at 91 14%Â©1 14%.
Liberal receipts and a large increase in the
world's visible supply made sentiment ln the
corn pit bearish. May opened unchanged to %c

closed at the opening price.

Wheat and Other Grains.
WHEAT âThe foreign markets showed no
particular change. Chicago was %c higher on
the day. The car shortage is now passing
away, as shown by the following dispatch
from Chicago:
"Practically all the Eastern roads were in
the grain trade yesterday, offering cars for
immediate loading. One shipping house re
ported to have been offered 200 cars and sim
ilar tenders were made to many. The Eastern
roads are beginning to get back the first rush
of cars sent forward early In December. From
a condition amounting practically to a block
ade appearance in the December rush to the
railroads we now see a period of dullness
ahead."
In this market futures were higher, with the
May shorts anxious to cover, but sellers scarce.
The cash grain was steady and unchanged.

Hay and FeedstutTs.
Rolled Barley is slightly higher with the
raw grain. Bran is In light supply and very
firm. The situation in Hay Is thus expressed
ln the circular of Somers & Co.:
"For the past two weeks receipts of Hay
have been very light, but 1950 tons arriving
last week and 1970 tons the week before. This
somewhat meager supply, however, has
sufficient to meet all demands, for throughout
the holidays trade has been quiet and there
has been but little of interest to report.
"During the last fortnight two vessels have
cleared for Oriental ports, taking perhaps twr
thousand tons of Hay. But stocks on storage
ln San Francisco have been heavy and these
shipments have had little effect other than to
reduce them, the shippers not having to go on
the market to secure the Hay. The United
States Government has just been in the market
for 1000 tons for prompt shipment, but even
this we think, will have little or no effect
on market conditions especially since the
entire State has had a good rain, giving us
some assurance of good crops next season.
"The United States Weather Bureau re
ports plowing and seeding still tn progress
and good prospects for a large acreage of Hay
and grain. Green feed Is still abundant and
with a continuance of favorable conditions we
cannot expect to see much of a change In
market conditions."

Potatoes, Onions and Vegetables.
The Potato market was firm for fancy of
ferings of table varieties, while off grades
continued to drag at irregular prices. Re
ceipts were moderate, but there was no scar
city of supplies, carrled-over stock being plen
tiful, and prices were without quotable change.
Onions were quiet and weak at the previously
quoted rates.
Receipts of southern vegetables were light
and Peas and Bean.<= were slightly firmer. To
matoes were still plentiful and slow of sale,
offerings being mostly poor. Green Peas ap
peared from Haywards and sold at 10c per
pound. Two sacks came in.

Poultry and Game.
Receipts of domestic Poultry were very light,
only 26 coops being received, and the matket
continued firm for all descriptions, with large
young chickens leading In point of demand.
A car of Western wss marketed, making the
fourth for the week and another car was
hourly expected. Eight cases of dressed Tur
keys came In and met with quick sale at
good prices. Aside from a slight decline in
prices of Sprig Duck, there were no changes
in Game, which met with a slugglih demand.

Butter, Cheese and Eggs.
The situation ln Butter remains about as re
ported early In the week. It Is weak and
dull, with stocks plentiful and buyers indiffer
ent and the market against the seller. Some
houses are disposed to reduce their creameries
lc to work off their stocks, while others con
tinue to quote the old figures. There Is no
snap whatever to the market. The medium
goods are lower. Cheese is easy and quiet.
Eggs are weak and declining, though the
downward tendency is gradual. Some houses
still quote 40c but they are ln the minority
and are getting fewer every day. Any posted
buyer can now buy fine ranch Eggs at 350
37 %c, and it takes a special Egg to bring over
39c. Stocks are not especially heavy, but. are
Increasing, and it is this that is depressing
the market.
Receipts were 21.400 lbs Butter, 662 cases
Eggs and 7800 lbs Cheese.

Deciduous and Citrus Fruits.
Trade in Oranges continued quiet yesterday
and jobbers manifested but little Interest In
the auction sale. Eight cars of Navels,
chiefly choice and fancy fruit, were offered
and less than one car was purchased by out
siders, the balance being bought ln by the
auction company. Following were the prices
obtained: Extra fancy Navels $1 5502; or
dinary fancy, $1 5001 75; choice. $1 2001 50;
standards, $1 08Â© 1 15. The other Citrus fruits
were quiet and featureless. The situation in
Apples was unchanged, the market being firm
and active. A carload fruit came in
from the Hood River district of Southern Ore
gon. Some fancy yellow Newtown Pippins
sold at $1 5001 75. but for the general market
$1 25 was still regarded as an extreme figure.
Strawberries have had their run and the quo
tations are withdrawn. Cranberries are get
ting nominal, as they are almost soid out.

STOCK MARKET.
Bjpnds Sell Fairly and Mining Shares
Active.
Local securities continued quiet, with no
charges worthy of note. Sales of bonds were
much better than those of stocks.
The oil and Tonopah mining shares were
quiet, the falling off in business in the latter
being abrupt and marked. The Com stock min
ing shares continued active, but prices were
lower than on the preceding day, as will be
noted.
The Seg Belcher Mining Company has levied
an assessment of sc.
The Ne\ada National Bank of San Francisco
has declared a regular quarterly dividend of
II 75 per share, or at the rate of 7 per cent
per annum, payable January 16.
The Chutes Company will pay the last of its
monthly dividends at the rate of 5" cents per
share to-day and thereafter the dividends will
be quarterly at the rate of 15 cents per share.
The regular annual meeting of the stockhold
ers of the Giant Powder Company Consolidated
has been called for January 25.
The regular annual meeting of the stockhold
ers of the Fireman's Fund Insurance Company
has been called for January 17.
The gross earnings c-f the United Railroads
of Ban Francisco for the month of November.
1904, were $567,673, an Increase of $34,106, as
compared with November, 1908. The gross
earnin-s for the eleven months ending No
vember 30, 1904. were $6,080,128, an increase
of 8397,296 over the corresponding period in
1903.
The Marina Marsicar.o Mining Company of
Shasta County has levied an assessment of 5
cent* per share, delinquent January 17.
Stocks quoted ex-dividend under the rules
yesterday were Bank ot California $4 per share
and California-street Cable Railroad 90 cents.
It is stated that tbe Union Oil Company will
this month pay a 7 per cent dividend and that
hereafter it will pay dividends at the rate of
6 per cent on par.

JURISTS HONOR
JUDGE LAWLOR
Is Unanimous Choice of Mem- j
bcrs of Superior Bench j
to Preside for This Year
o
SECRETARY NOT CHOSEN
Two Candidates for Place
Vacated by Coffroth Are
Short of Needed Votes:
Â«
Judge William P. Lawior was unani- |
mously chosen as presiding Judge of
the Superior Court for the ensuing yeaf
at a meeting of his fellow jurists held
yesterday afternoon in Judge Kerri
gan's chambers. Judge Sloss was the
only absentee and the eleven others
could not agree upon the selection of a
successor to James W. Coffroth. whose
resignation as secretary of the court
was accepted. There were six votes
fur John F. Tyler, court commissioner
and librarian of the law library, and
five votes for Court Commissioner
Thomas S- Mulvey. An adjournment
was taken to 1:30 this afternoon, at
which time Judge Sloss will be present, !
and either Mulvey or Tyler will be
elected. In either event a new court,
commissioner will have to be ap- j
pointed.
The Judges decided to follow the ,
usual course in calling grand jurors
and 144 will be named, each Judge se
lectins twelve. It was also decided to ,
call -00 citizens to serve on the petit ;
juries.
Judge Lawior has issued a notice ;
that business of the court requiring â 
the action of the presiding Judge will
hereafter be transacted at the Hall of
Justice between tho hours of 9 a. m. !
and 8:o0 p. tn. Actions and proceed- ;
lugs filed between the hours of 8:30
in tho morning and 1 in the afternoon
v. ill be assigned at 2 p. m. of the same
day and those filed after 1 o'clock will
be assigned at 9 the next morning. In
cases where the filing could not be
made reasonably with reference to the
hours indicated for the making of as
signments application may be made to
the presiding Judge to file In the
branch office of the County Clerk at
the Hall of Justice. This will apply
to proceedings for the issuance of
extraordinary writs or other urgent
relief.

possible the reclamation of a large i

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JANUARY 5, 1905.
ATHLETIC MANIA
ROUTS PEACE.
Invades an Apartment House
and Tranquillity Flees.
First-Floor Matron Hales Scion of
Second Floor Into Court. -
A more tranquil apartment-house
than 3637 Nineteenth street was not
in San Francisco when a scion of the
family of Newberger, occupying the
second story, became possessed of a
mania for indulgence in indoor ath
letics.' From that moment comfort
fled the home of the Gfrerers, on th*
first floor. If a quartz mill or a boil
er factory had been in operation over*
; head the Gfrerers' peace could not
j have been more routed.
Such was the complaint of Mrs,
! Lizzie Gfrerer, who appeared before
! Police Judge Fritz as prosecutrix ol
I 18-year-old Will Newberger. and'ehe
had about half a dozen feminine heads
of other families resident at 3637
Nineteenth street to testify that she
did not exaggerate her grievance. She
made young Will the defendant be
cause she had reason to believe that
he was the chief offender. It was his
dally and nightly wont, she averred,
to acquire wind and muscle by means
|of punching the air bag, skipping the
j rope, tossing the medicine ball, clout
ing the sand sack, wrestling and box
ing with shadowy or real adversaries
and performing various and sundry
other violent physical exercises that
vibrated the floor and made her dread
that any moment the ceiling might de
scend and crush her, to say nothing of
| the aural annoyance caused by the
trampling and shuffling of the youth'j
, feet. It was not until she had ex
| hausted remonstrance that the law's
I aid was solicited by her. No, she was
I not opposed to the pursuit of physical
; culture, either as a hygienic measure
{or as a means of pastime, but she be
' lieved there was reason in all things
I and that the student, of athletics
[ should be compelled to consider the
convenience of other folk as well as
i his own enjoyment. It was Master
l Newberger's right to promote h's
I health and upbuild his physique, but
i rot at the expense of his neighbors'
| nerves. Why didn't he establish his
gymnasium where it would not be a
' constant source of irritation to every
j other person in the same building?
| Then the Newbergers informed the
i court that in 3637 Nineteenth street
there were more harassing things than
! Will's athletic performancesâMrs.
i Gfrerer's tongue, for exampleâwhich
I they had witnesses to prove. But none
,of the waiting and willing witnesses
for either side was called. After sur
; veylng the two groups of ladies ihe
court inquired if the landlord of 3637
were present and when that gentle
-1 man strode to the bench and bowed
j his Honor said:
"I'll have to ( pass the task pf set
j tling this contention up to you. As
the owner of the house you know
which of the partiesâthe Newbergers
or the Gfrerersâyou prefer as ten
ants, and I would suggest that you rii
your premises of the least desirable
of the two families as speedily as pos
sible. For It is apparent to me that
the breach between them is un
bridgeable and that no house can
harbor both of them and quietude."
The landlord's acquiescent nods in
dicated that he shared the Judge's
opinion.
â¢ â¢ â¢
Joseph Smith, charged with the mur
der of Joseph McGowan, told his ver
sion of the tragedy to Judge Mogan.
He disclaimed recollection of the actual
killing, but remembered that McGowan
and he had wordy altercation ere the
latter entered the yard in which the
shooting occurred.
"Why don't you remember the shoot
ing?" the Judge Inquired.
"Did you never see a man knocked
out?" was the answer, delivered with
show of* temper. "I had been drink
ing and was dazed. No, I don't re
member how many drinks I took that
dayâdon't know whether I took ten or
a hundred."
Several times the attorney for the
defense, ex-Judge D. J. Murphy, ob
jected to the prosecution's line of
questioning in the course of cross
examination, and on each occasion the
defendant angrily declared that he
wanted to answer. Finally the Judge
told him that his manner was "very
offensive" and did not tend to help his
case.
"Well, I don't like to have these attor
neys interrupting and stopping me
when I want to speak," waa the surly
response.
The cross-examination will be re
sumed this morning.
â¢ â¦ â¢
Autchell Zimmerman was accused by
Mrs. Anna Damenstein of having dis
turbed the peace of her dwelling, 1049
Folsom street, by going there with one
Mr. Rubenstein and indulging in such
loud and threatening language that Mr.
Rubenstein implored him to refrain
from striking young Ikey Blumenstein,
who had sought refuge under Mrs.
Damenstein's kitchen table. The object
of Mr. Zimmerman's visit to the house
of Damenstein was to ascertain
whether it was Mr. or Mrs. Zimmerman
who had been accused by Frank
Damenstein of owing him $10, and while
in pursuit" of such enlightenment, it
BY JAMES C. CRAWFORD.
was alleged by Mrs. Damenstein, Mr.
Zimmerman lost his,temper, terrorized
the unoffending Master Blumenstein
and provoked reproof from Mr. Ruben
stein. Judge Cabaniss has given him
self thirty days to study out the
nomenclatural phase of the problem.
â¢ â¢ â¢
Lillian Lucas, a favorite of stage and
box at the MJdwav Plaisance, wore a
black-and-white check tailor-made and
a black picture hat and was accom
panied by her mother, a portly lady,
when she appeared before Judge Fritz
as co-defendant with George Kennedy,
a waiter at the same playhouse, on the
charge of having drugged and robbed
W. A. Williams, an Arizonan, of $1400
last Sunday night. Mr. Williams, a
little man with an ascetic face, had a
beclouded recollection of what hap
pened after he had absorbed a few
beverages ordered by the fair Lillian
at his. expense. He would not swear
that either she or the waiter took his
money. All he was positive of was
that it was missing when he awoke in
a Fourth street lodging-house, whither
he was transferred from the theater
in a cab ordered by Kennedy. The
hearing was continued till this morn
ing and the woman's bail reduced from
$3QOO to $1500 by Judge Fritz.
c c â¢
James Kelly, born of full-blooded
Ethiopian parents in Limerick, Ire
land, made his seventh appearance be
fore Judge Mogan on the same old
charge of vagrancy and was given
three months' imprisonment after he
had despairingly daunted a ferry ticket
as documentary proof of his intent to
immediately leave for Oakland if tho
court would restore him to freedom.
Bailiff Hlckey, who also first saw light
on the banks of the Shannon, chuckled
with malignant glee as he shoved the
despondent man of color back into the
cage.
The arresting policeman testified that
he scooped In Mr. Kelly from the post
office, where he was extending compli
ments of the holiday season and en
deavoring to obtain a gratuity of one
dime from each person who was so
indiscreet as to hearken to him. In his
mixed Hibernian and negro dialect Mr.
Kelly would say:
"De top ob th' mor-r-nln' till ye, en
shure it's meself wishes yo' a happy
Noo Yeah, sah, en may th' shadow ob
ye nivir grow liss till ye've libbed ter
see a hundhred more ob thim.
Mor-r-re power till ye, sah, en cud
ye lind me th' loan ob a dime?"
Mr. iKelly denied that he was prac
â¢* " ' â+

KOWALSKY FAST
WITH HIS FISTS
Attorney Participates in
Lively Encounter With Ar
thur Meadows in Court
LANDS BLOW ON FACE
Belligerents Apologize and
Justice of Peace Lawson
Reads Them Harsh Lecture !
Colonel Henry L Kowalsky and
| Arthur Meadows figured in a lively en- ]
counter yesterday in the courtroom of
Justice of the Peace Lawson, in which
Meadows came out second best. The j
Incident occurred just before the trial I
of the suit of J*. A. Miller, as Meadows' j
assignee, to recover $299 99 from Ko
walsky, alleged to be due from certain j
patent letter street signs. The court
room was full of spectators waiting for j
Judge Lawson to appear.
Kowalsky and Meadows were seated
near each other and began to discuss
the case. Kowahjky said something
which angered Meadows, who there
upon reviled Kowalsky and at
the same time rushed threateningly
toward him. The latter, despite his
300 pounds avoirdupois, was too quick,
however. Rising quickly, he gave
Meadows a blow between the eyes
straight from the shoulder. Blood
streamed from Meadows' face and a
lively scrimmage ensued, in which sev- I
eral bystanders took part. Women J
screamed and Judge Lawson rushed
In from his chambers, when the dis- '
turbance ended as if by magic. Law- I
son immediately ordered the belliger- j
ents in the custody of a Sheriff's dep- j
uty. Meadows apologized to the ladies |
present for his language and Kowalsky
seid it was the first time during twen- I
ty-four years' practice that he had fig
tired in such an experience, which
was all the more to be regretted as
he is about to depart for Europe for
an indefinite visit. He said the provo
cation was great, but asked that Judge i
Lawson mete out whatever punish- ,
ment he deemed just. Lawson read 1
the offenders a severe lecture and
afterward dismissed Miller's suit with- i
out prejudice.

PROVES KEELEY
WAS IN HOUSE
Police Serjeant Sylvester
j Gives Valuable Evidence
i in the Metcalfe Inquest
. i
Important testimony was given yes
terday by Police Sergeant Daniel Syl- |
vester at the inquest on the body of
Edward Bruce Metcalfe, who was shot
and killed 1 at 18 Seventh street on the
morning of December 29, presumably
ty Edward Pierce Keeley, the para
mour of the dead man's wife. Mrs.
Annie Metcalfe, the widow, had Just
testified denying that she had told the
police that Keeley did the shooting.
She said that she supposed it might
have been Keeley.
Sergeant Sylvester said that he
found Metcalfe dead on the floor of
room 8 and Mrs. Metcalfe sitting on the
edge of the bed in room 6. She was
attired only in her undershirt- The'
lower part of her body was covered
with a bedspread, and on the spread
was the bloody imprint of a bare foot.
The witness said that he talked with
the woman in that room for an hour
and a half or two hours and that she
told him that Keeley had shot her hvfc
band. She had first inquired whether
her husband was dead and when told
that he was she declared that she
would not tell the witness anything.
When he asked her where the revolver
was Mrs. Metcalfe replied that he
should catch Keeley and probably he
would find where the weapon was.
The witness then testified that he
went to 603 Eddy street, where Keeley
and Mrs. Metcalfe had roomed. There
he was informed by two persons whose
names he declined to divulge that
shortly after the shooting Keeley had
come to his room and told them that
he had killed Metcalfe. They said also
that Keeley changed his clothing for
a blouse and overalls and left the
he use.
The witness added tnat he had
learned that the revolver had been
given to Keeley by a man who was'l
afraid that his wife would commit sui
cide and who therefore did not like to
have the weapon lying around.
Mr. and Mrs. Charles T. Byrne, who
occupied a room directly opposite that
in which Metcalfe had been killed, tes
tified that they heard the sound of
angry voices, a scuffle, the report of a
pistol and footsteps of some one run
ning down the hall. Mrs. Byrne found
Mrs. Metcalfe sitting on the bed near
her husband's body. When she asked
Mrs. Metcalfe what" was the matter
she received a profane reply. Then*
Mrs- Metcalfe wrapped a sheet about
her and went into the hall.
The inquest will be resumed this
morning.
TWO COUPLES DISCOVER
UNWELCOME THIRD SPOUSE
Annulments Sought by Wives Who
Were in IgnoranceâNew Divorce
Cases Begun.
In the Superior Court yesterday Al
ice Spurway asked for divorce from
George Spurway. whom she married
last June, on the ground that she had
discovered that he has another wife
living. Ada M. Moorhead Jordan
asks that her union with Robert J.
Jordan, which took place in October,
1897, be set aside because her first
husband is still in the land of the liv
ing.
Gilbert Thompson asks for a di
vorce because his wife. Mamie, is too
fond of intoxicating liquors and has
been cruel to him. He says that six
weeks after their marriage she at
tacked him with a paring knife and
threw a pot of hot tea' upon him.
Other suits for separation were begun
by Katy W. Morrison against Angus
B. Morrison, desertion; Florence W.
Brome against Joseph A. Brome, neg
lect.
Judge Kerrigan granted a divorce
to Josephine Peck from. Peter Peck,
who was convicted of a felony. He
got eighteen months for injuring the
jail at Santa Cruz.
